The problem is that thread_col differs by 16, and 16 * TN = 16 * 2 = 32, which is exactly the number of banks. Any multiple of 32 in the column address causes wrapping. To fix this, we'd need to ensure that thread_cols that differ by 16 don't map to the same bank. This means we need the bank function to NOT be periodic with period 32 for columns spaced by 32. If B is stored as Bs[col][kk] with Bs_width = 33 (or something): Bank = (col * 33 + kk) % 32 = (col + kk) % 32. For col = thread_col * 2: bank = (2 * thread_col + kk) % 32. Same issue. If Bs_width is even larger, modulo 32 still dominates. OK, I think 2-way bank conflicts are unavoidable with TN=2 and 64 thread columns. But they're relatively mild, and since shared memory has high bandwidth, the performance impact should be small. Let me also check the A read again. With As_width = 17: As[(thread_row * 32 + i) * 17 + kk] For thread_row = 0 and varying i: Address = i * 17 + kk Bank = (i * 17 + kk) % 32 For i=0: kk i=1: 17+kk i=2: 2+kk i=3: 19+kk i=4: 4+kk i=5: 21+kk i=6: 6+kk i=7: 23+kk i=8: 8+kk i=9: 25+kk i=10: 10+kk i=11: 27+kk i=12: 12+kk i=13: 29+kk i=14: 14+kk i=15: 31+kk i=16: 16+kk i=17: 1+kk i=18: 18+kk i=19: 3+kk i=20: 20+kk i=21: 5+kk i=22: 22+kk i=23: 7+kk i=24: 24+kk i=25: 9+kk i=26: 26+kk i=27: 11+kk i=28: 28+kk i=29: 13+kk i=30: 30+kk i=31: 15+kk For a fixed kk, banks for i=0..31 are all UNIQUE! For i=1, all read bank 17+kk. etc. But within the warp, all threads are reading the SAME address (since thread_row is the same for all threads in a warp). This is a broadcast! No bank conflict because all threads in the warp access the same address, and the hardware handles this in a single transaction. Broadcasts are actually the best case for shared memory access. So A reads are perfect. The only issue is B reads with 2-way conflicts. Let me see if I can do better. What if I make Bs_width = 33? What if I reorder the elements in shared memory? A common swizzle for GPUs is: swizzled_col = (col / 4) * 32 + (col % 4) * 8 + (col / 32) % 8 This ensures that consecutive threads in a warp access consecutive banks. But implementing this adds complexity. Let me see if a simple swizzle helps. For TN=2, thread_col=0..63. Thread 0 accesses cols 0,1. Thread 16 accesses cols 32,33. Actually, the conflict isn't about adjacency; it's that any two addresses 32 apart map to the same bank. In a warp of 32 threads, since there are 4 thread rows, each of the 16 thread_cols in 0..15 appears twice (for 2 thread rows). Wait, let me recalculate. Actually, 256 threads = 8 warps (32 threads each). thread_row = threadIdx.x / 64 = 0,1,2,3 thread_col = threadIdx.x % 64 = 0..63 Warp 0: threads 0-31 - thread_row = 0 (for all 32 threads) - thread_col = 0..31 - Thread_col values 0-31 all appear once. Warp 1: threads 32-63 - thread_row = 0 - thread_col = 32..63 Warp 2: threads 64-95 - thread_row = 1 - thread_col = 0..31 Warp 3: threads 96-127 - thread_row = 1 - thread_col = 32..63 Etc. For warp 0: thread_cols 0-31. For j=0: cols 0, 2, 4, ..., 62. Thread_col 0: col 0, bank kk Thread_col 1: col 2, bank kk+2 Thread-col 16: col 32, bank kk+0 ← same as thread_col 0! So in warp 0, thread_col 0 and 16 both read bank kk. 16 threads per warp, so 2 threads per bank group. 2-way conflict. In warp 1: thread_cols 32-63. Thread_col 32: col 64, bank kk+0 (64%32=0) Thread_col 48: col 96, bank kk+0 (96%32=0) But thread_col 32 and 48 are in the same warp. Same 2-way conflict. This is unavoidable because any 32 consecutive even columns (spaced by 2) will have pairs that differ by 32, mapping to the same bank. OK, 2-way conflicts are the best we can do for TN=2 unless we drastically change the approach. Now, 2-way conflicts mean the memory access takes 2x as long. Is that acceptable? Shared memory bandwidth on H100 is ~15 TB/s per SM (approximately). A 2-way conflict reduces effective bandwidth to ~7.5 TB/s. Still very fast. For compute: - 32 A reads per kk, all broadcasts (1 transaction each). 32 transactions. - 2 B reads per kk, 2-way conflicts (2 transactions each). 4 transactions. - Total: 36 transactions per kk. - For BK=16: 576 transactions per k-tile. - At 2 cycles per transaction (roughly): ~1152 cycles. Compute: 32 * 2 = 64 FMAs per kk. At 1 FMA per cycle per CUDA core (simplified), that's 64 cycles. But FMAs can be pipelined. The memory latency can be hidden by pipelining. With 25% occupancy (2 blocks per SM, 512 threads = 16 warps per SM), there should be enough warps to hide latency. Actually, this is getting very detailed. Let me just write the kernel and test it. One more thing: for the shared memory size, I need to be careful. At 17 KB per block and 2 blocks per SM, that's 34 KB per SM. H100 has 228 KB shared memory per SM. Plenty of room. Actually, let me reduce BK from 16 to 8 to lower shared memory usage. But with BK=8, there are more k-tile iterations (512 instead of 256), increasing loop overhead. BK=16 should be a good balance. Wait, BK=16 means 4096/16 = 256 iterations. With good unrolling, the loop overhead is minimal. Also, for BK=16, the A tile is 128x16 = 2,048 floats. B tile is 16x128 = 2,048 floats. Total = 4,096 floats = 16 KB. With padding (129*16 + 128*17... wait): As: 128 * 17 floats = 2,176 floats = 8,704 bytes Bs: 16 * 129 floats = 2,064 floats = 8,256 bytes Total: 16,960 bytes ≈ 16.6 KB That's fine. If I use BK=32: As: 128 * 33 = 4,224 floats = 16,896 bytes Bs: 32 * 129 = 4,128 floats = 16,512 bytes Total: 33,408 bytes = 32.6 KB Still fine for one block. But with BK=32, each k-tile does more compute (32 iterations of the kk loop), reducing the percentage of time spent on loads. Let me try BK=32 for potentially better compute intensity. With BK=32: - Outer loop iterations: 4096/32 = 128 - Inner kk loop: 32 iterations - FMAs per kk: 64 - Total FMAs per k-tile: 32 * 64 = 2,048 - Total FMAs: 128 * 2,048 = 262,144. Correct. More unrolling, better pipelining. I'll use BK=32. Register count doesn't change since it's based on TM and TN, not BK. Actually, with BK=32, the b0 and b1 preloads still happen once per kk. The a_val is loaded 32 times per k-tile instead of 16. The total loop is half as long, which reduces loop overhead. Let me go with BK=32. One more optimization: since N=4096 is known at compile time for the benchmark, I can use template arguments or just hardcode the values. Hardcoding is easier and allows better compiler optimization. Let me write the kernel now. "}<|tool_call_end|><|tool_calls_section_end|>
